Does the Ba Indicate Partiality? A Hanafi-Ashafi'i Usul Dispute
Dispute over the function of ba in Quranic wiping verses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on the function of the 'ba' in wiping verses, apply the Hanafi linguistic analysis to derive the partial wiping requirement for the head, and evaluate the evidence for full coverage in tayammum.

Core Idea
The 'ba' in wiping verses does not itself mean partiality; partiality is derived from the verb's transitivity to the tool.

The preposition 'ba' in the Quranic command 'wamsahu bi ru'usikum' is the focus of a classic usul dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. Does it indicate that only part of the head must be wiped, or does it serve a different grammatical function? This passage guides us through the Hanafi linguistic reasoning, showing how the structure of the verb leads to a conclusion about the obligatory amount. It also extends the discussion to tayammum, where a different principle applies. By mastering this analysis, you will sharpen your ability to derive rulings from Quranic language using Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested linguistic point
The word 'ba' in the verse is claimed by some (including al-Shafi'i) to indicate partiality (tab'id). The Hanafi school disagrees. Note the precise claim and the counterclaim.
This is about the linguistic meaning of a single letter, not about the fiqh ruling itself.
2
Understand the Hanafi's own derivation of partiality
The text argues that partiality comes from the fact that the verb 'mash' is transitive to the tool (the hand), and the head (locus) becomes like a tool via the 'ba'. Therefore, only part of the tool is needed to achieve the goal – wiping some of the head. Three fingers is the minimum.
The derivation relies on the concept of 'tools' which do not need full coverage.
3
Contrast with the Shafi'i position and its implications
Al-Shafi'i held that the 'ba' itself denotes tab'id. The Hanafi response explains why this is incorrect – if 'ba' meant partiality, the verse would specify a fraction, but it does not. The Hanafi derivation avoids this problem.
This step helps you see how ijtihad differs: same text, different ijtihad.
4
Follow the expansion: the quarter of the head via sunnah
The text introduces a second narration from Abu Hanifa that the verse is ambiguous (mujmal) regarding quantity, and the Prophet's act (wiping the forelock) clarifies it as a quarter. This shows how sunnah can specify a mujmal text.
Note the difference between the two Hanafi positions – one uses linguistic analysis, the other uses ambiguity+sunnah.
5
Examine the tayammum exception and its principle
For tayammum, the Hanafi position (Zahir al-Riwayah) requires full coverage of face and hands, despite the same 'ba' structure. Why? Because tayammum is a substitute for wudu, and the sunnah confirms full coverage. The 'ba' is treated as redundant (zā'idah). Contrast the two derivations.
This shows the principle of substitution (khalaf) overriding a linguistic analysis.

الآلة، فكأنه قيل مسحت اليد بالحائط فيشبه المحل بالوسائل في أخذ بعضه. فلا يقتضي استيعاب الرأس، وإنما يقتضي إلصاق الآلة بالمحل، وذلك لا يستوعب الكل عادة، فصار المراد به أكثر اليد، وذلك مقدار ثلاث أصابع؛ لأن الأصابع أصل في اليد والكف تابع والثلاث أكثرها، فأقيم مقام الكل.

لأنه لم يعلم أن المراد كل الرأس أو بعضه، فيكون فعل النبي أنه هو مسح على ناصيته بيانًا له، والناصية هي مقدار ربع الرأس، فيكون مسح ربع الرأس فرضا، سواء كان بثلاث أصابع أو كلها؛ لأن الكلام فيها طويل، وإنما يثبت استيعاب مسح الوجه واليد في التيمم لقوله تعالى: ﴿فَامْسَحُوا بِوُجُوهِكُمْ وَأَيْدِيكُمْ ؛ لأنه خلف عن الوضوء، فيعامل
(النساء: ٤٣)
معاملته في الوجه واليد؛ ولأنه ثبت الاستيعاب فيه بالسنة المشهورة،
للرأس.(القمر)
مقدار ربع الرأس فإن الناصية هي أحد الجوانب الأربع للرأس. (القمر) لأن الكلام فيها متعلق بقوله : ولم يتعرض إلخ والله أعلم ماذا أراد الشارح الله بالكلام، وما يختلج في القلب :هو أنا لا نسلم أولاً أن الآية مجملة ولو كانت محملة لتوقف السلف من الصحابة والتابعين في الاستدلال بها، و لم ينقل التوقف ولو سلم أن الآية بجملة، فنقول: إن الباء في حديث المغيرة داخلة على الناصية كما مر في رواية "مسلم"، وإذا دخلت الباء على المحل يشبه بالوسائل فيراد البعض لا الكل فما لزم منه إلا مسح بعض الناصية لا كل الناصية، فكيف يثبت مسح ربع الرأس فتدبر . (القمر) وإنما يثبت إلخ جواب سؤال مقدر تقريره أن الباء في آية التيمم داخلة على المحل والفعل متعد إلى الآلة، والتقدير: فامسح الأيدي بوجوهكم وأيديكم، فينبغي على قاعدتكم أنه لا يستوعب مسح الوجه واليد في التيمم مع أنكم قلتم ،باستيعابه ثم اعلم أن شرط الاستيعاب في التيمم إنما هو على ظاهر الرواية، وأما على رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة له باستيعابه ليس بشرط بل يكفي مسح الأكثر من كل عضو، وقال شمس الأئمة الحلواني: ينبغي أن يحفظ رواية الحسن جدًا لكثرة البلوى فيه كذا في "ذخيرة العقبى". (القمر) لأنه خلف إلخ: أي لأن التيمم خلف عن الوضوء، فإن قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِن كُنتُمْ مَرْضَى أَوْ عَلَى سَفَرٍ أَوْ جَاءَ أَحَدٌ مِنْكُمْ مِنَ الْغَائِطِ أَوْ لامَسْتُمُ النِّسَاءَ فَلَمْ تَجِدُوا مَاءً فَتَيَمَّمُوا صَعِيداً طَيِّبا (النساء : ٤٣) إلى صريح في بيان الخلفية، فيعامل معاملة الوضوء في الوجه واليد فكما أن استيعاب غسل الوجه واليد في الوضوء ضروري، فكذا استيعاب مسحهما في التيمم يكون ضروريًا، فالباء في الآية صلة أي زائدة. (القمر) ولأنه إلخ : معطوف على قوله : لأنه إلخ. (القمر) بالسنة المشهورة أي لا ،بالآية فالسنة المشهورة بيان للآية في حق الاستيعاب، فجعلت الباء في الآية صلة زائدة بدلالة الحديث المشهور. (القمر)
أخرج مسلم في "صحيحه" رقم ٢٧٤ ، باب المسح على الناصية والعمامة عن المغيرة بن شعبة أن النبي توضأ فمسح بناصيته وعلى العمامة وعلى الخفين.
Statement of the divisions of the third division. 376. Discussion of 'Ba'. What achieves the objective. And when it enters the locus of wiping, the verb remains transitive to the tool, as if it is said: 'I wiped with the wall' or it is said 'and wipe your heads', then at that time the wiping becomes transitive to the tool. It is as if it was said 'I wiped the hand with the wall' so the locus resembles the means in taking part of it. So it does not necessitate complete coverage of the head; rather it necessitates attaching the tool to the locus, and that does not usually cover the whole. So what is intended by it is most of the hand, and that is the amount of three fingers; because the fingers are the origin in the hand and the palm is subordinate, and three is most of them, so it stands in place of the whole. So partialness is intended through this way, not as al-Shafi'i claimed that the ba is for partialness. This is from one of the two narrations from Abu Hanifa, and he did not address the other narration, which is that it is ambiguous regarding the quantity. What achieves the objective, because the tool is not intended but rather it is an intermediary between the agent and the object in conveying the effect to it, and the locus is the intended in the transitive verb. So complete coverage of the tool is not required; rather, what achieves the objective suffices. (al-Muhashi) 'The objective' etc.: Know that wiping is touching with the inner palm; so the hand is the tool and the wiped object is the locus of the action, and that which is considered in the tool is what achieves the objective; so complete coverage is not required in it. (al-Sunbuli) 'To the tool' i.e., not to the locus, for the locus then is in the genitive case due to the ba. (al-Qamar) 'It is as if it was said' etc.: As if it was said: 'and wipe the hands on your heads.' (al-Qamar) 'So the locus resembles the means' etc.: That is, when the ba enters the locus, and by it is meant the wiped object as explained earlier, it becomes similar to the tool. So its complete coverage is not required either, just as complete coverage of the tool is not required; because the objective is to attach the action and to affirm the attribute of attachment in the action, so the action becomes intended for affirming the attribute of attachment and the locus is a means to it. So what suffices is the amount by which the objective is achieved, namely attaching the action to the head, and that is achieved with part of the head. So partialness is derived from this, not from the linguistic meaning. (al-Sunbuli) 'So it does not necessitate' etc.: Because the verb is not attached to the head. (al-Qamar) 'It does not require the whole': i.e., the whole of the tool usually, for what is between the fingers of the hand is difficult to affix. (al-Qamar) 'An amount of three fingers': It is not permissible to wipe with two fingers or one finger; thus in 'Rasa'il al-Arkan'. (al-Qamar) 'Origin in the hand': The fingers are the origin in gripping and striking; hence half the diyah is obligatory for cutting all five fingers without the palm, just as half the diyah is obligatory for cutting the five fingers with the palm; thus it is said. (al-Qamar) 'This way': i.e., by way of the verb being transitive to the tool. (al-Qamar) 'Ambiguous' etc.: Because estimating the object (i.e., 'the hands') for His saying 'and wipe' (5:6) is contrary to the principle; so the verb is made like an intransitive verb; thus the meaning is 'perform wiping of the head', so the verse becomes ambiguous regarding the quantity. (al-Qamar) Page 378. Statement of the divisions of the third division. 377. 'Aliyah. Discussion of 'Ba'. Because it is not known whether the intended is all of the head or part of it; so the action of the Prophet that he wiped over his forelock clarifies it, and the forelock is the amount of a quarter of the head; so wiping a quarter of the head becomes obligatory, whether it is with three fingers or all of them; because the discussion about it is lengthy. Complete coverage of wiping the face and hands in tayammum is established because of His saying 'and wipe your faces and hands' (4:43); because it is a substitute for wudu, so it is treated like wudu regarding the face and hand; and because complete coverage is established in it by the well-known sunnah. For the head. (al-Qamar) 'Amount of a quarter of the head': The forelock is one of the four sides of the head. (al-Qamar) 'Because the discussion about it is related to his statement: 'and he did not address' etc. And Allah knows best what the commentator meant by the discussion. What comes to mind is that we do not first accept that the verse is ambiguous; and if it were ambiguous, the Salaf among the Companions and Followers would have hesitated in using it as evidence, and hesitation is not reported. And if we accept that the verse is ambiguous, we say: The ba in the hadith of Mughirah enters upon the forelock as mentioned in the narration of Muslim. And when the ba enters the locus, it resembles the means so part is intended, not all. So nothing is necessitated except wiping part of the forelock, not all of the forelock. So how is wiping a quarter of the head established? So ponder. (al-Qamar) 'And complete coverage is established' etc. is the answer to an estimated question: The ba in the verse of tayammum enters the locus and the verb is transitive to the tool, and the implied meaning is 'wipe the hands on your faces and hands'. So according to your principle, it would be that complete coverage of the face and hand in tayammum is not required, yet you have said it is required. Then know that the condition of complete coverage in tayammum is only according to the apparent narration. As for the narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa, complete coverage is not a condition; rather, wiping the majority of each limb suffices. And Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani said: 'The narration of al-Hasan should be preserved much due to the frequent difficulty (balwa) in it.' Thus in 'Dhakhir al-Uqba'. (al-Qamar) 'Because it is a substitute' etc.: Because tayammum is a substitute for wudu. For His saying 'if you are ill or on a journey or one of you comes from the toilet or you have touched women and you find no water, then perform tayammum with clean earth' (4:43) is explicit in indicating substitution. So it is treated like wudu regarding the face and hands. So just as complete coverage of washing the face and hands in wudu is necessary, similarly complete coverage of wiping them in tayammum becomes necessary. So the ba in the verse is a connective (sila), i.e., redundant. (al-Qamar) 'And because' etc. is coordinated with 'because' etc. (al-Qamar) 'By the well-known sunnah': i.e., not by the verse; the well-known sunnah explains the verse regarding complete coverage. So the ba in the verse is rendered as a redundant connective (sila) by the indication of the well-known hadith. Muslim narrated in his Sahih (no. 274, chapter on wiping over the forelock and turban) from al-Mughira ibn Shu'ba that the Prophet performed wudu and wiped over his forelock and over the turban and over the khuffs.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools over the meaning of the preposition 'ba' (ب) in the Quranic verse of wiping the head (5:6). The Hanafi position, as derived here, is that 'ba' does not itself indicate partiality (tab'id). Instead, partiality is inferred from the grammatical structure: the verb 'wiping' is transitive to the tool (the hand), and the head is the locus (mahall). When 'ba' enters the locus, the verb remains transitive to the tool, meaning the tool (hand) does the action on the locus. Since the tool only needs to achieve the objective (attaching the action to the head), it is sufficient to wipe part of the head. Shafi'i held that 'ba' directly indicates partiality, but Hanafi scholars argue that this is not from the linguistic meaning but from the reasoning above. The text also discusses a second narration from Abu Hanifa that the verse is ambiguous (mujmal) regarding the quantity, and the Prophet's practice (wiping the forelock) clarifies it to be one quarter. Then the discussion shifts to tayammum: the verse (4:43) uses 'ba' on the face and hands. Hanafi position in the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah) requires complete coverage because tayammum is a substitute (khalaf) for wudu, so it inherits the ruling of complete coverage. However, there is a narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad (Nawadir) that only the majority of each limb suffices. The text notes that Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani recommended preserving this latter narration due to practical difficulty. The hadith of Mughirah (Muslim) is used to show that the Prophet wiped his forelock (part of head) with 'ba' entering the locus, supporting the Hanafi view that partial wiping is sufficient.

Key Terms
الباء (al-ba): The preposition 'ba' in the Quranic verses; the text argues it does not inherently indicate partiality but rather serves to make the verb transitive to the tool, implying partiality indirectly.
التبعيض (al-tab'id): Partiality or indicating a part of the whole. The text explains that this is derived from the verb's transitivity to the tool, not from the ba itself.
المسح (al-mash): Wiping, defined as touching with the inner palm (batin al-kaf). It is a light action that does not require flow of water.
الآلة (al-alah): The tool; in the context of wiping, the hand is the tool (alah) that performs the wiping.
المحل (al-mahall): The locus; in the verse, the head (or face/hands for tayammum) is the locus where the wiping action is applied.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi analysis in the passage, what is the source of partiality (tab'id) in the verse 'wamsahu bi ru'usikum'?
A) The preposition 'ba' itself indicates partiality.
B) The verb is transitive to the tool, making the head like a tool, so only part of it is needed.
C) The Prophet's hadith of wiping the forelock explicitly says to wipe a quarter.
D) The verse is ambiguous, so we must take the minimum.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul teaches not to assign arbitrary meanings to particles like 'ba', but to derive rulings from the full grammatical structure of the verse and the established practices of the Prophet, using principles like verb transitivity and substitution.

Al-Quran as Source
intermediate
8 min
Reasoning by Explicit Text: 'Ibarat al-Nass and Its Classification
Introduces the fourth division: istidlal bi 'ibarat al-nass and its classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why istidlal bi 'ibarat al-nass is counted among the divisions of nazm by tolerance and apply the principle that the kaf of comparison makes a statement explicit (sarih) rather than kinayah.

Core Idea
Reasoning by explicit text is an act of the jurist, discussed under nazm only by tolerance.

This lesson examines the fourth division of textual structure: reasoning by the explicit wording of a text ('ibarat al-nass). The author clarifies that although 'istidlal' (reasoning) is an activity of the mujtahid, it is included here for convenience because it directly engages with the text's apparent meaning. Through the example of the kaf of comparison, we see how a statement that might seem ambiguous can become explicit and definitive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Division and Its Definition
Locate the heading 'بيان عبارة النص' and read the definition: 'العمل بظاهر ما سيق الكلام له' (acting upon the apparent meaning for which the speech was intended). Recognize that this is the simplest form of textual reasoning.
This is the default method; the other divisions (isharah, nass, iqtida) are more complex.
2
Understand the Tolerance (Tasamuh)
The author states that counting reasoning among the divisions of nazm is by tolerance (tasamuhan) because reasoning is an act of the one who reasons (fa'l al-mustadill), not a property of the text itself. Appreciate the methodological distinction: the text has structure; reasoning is the jurist's work.
This shows careful usul thinking: we must not confuse ontology of the text with epistemology of the jurist.
3
Analyze the Example of the Kaf of Comparison
The passage discusses the case where a third party says 'huwa kama qulta' (it is as you said) after someone accuses another of zina. The kaf (as) creates generality ('umum). In Hanafi usul, a general word is definitive (qat'i) in covering all its instances. Hence, the third party's statement is not kinayah but sarih (explicit), and he is liable to hadd.
The statement of 'Ali about dhimmis is cited to support that the kaf brings generality even in matters where doubts repel punishment.
4
Distinguish 'Ibarat al-Nass from Kinayah
Because the kaf creates generality and the general is definitive, the statement ceases to be kinayah. Recognize that 'ibarat al-nass is the explicit intended meaning; anything less is either kinayah or requires other methods.
This distinction is crucial for applying hudud punishments where doubt repels.
5
Note the Authority Level
The passage is from a matn with glosses. The ruling on the kaf is based on the statement of 'Ali (a companion), which is a source of authority in Hanafi usul. However, it is zanni in transmission and meaning, so the rule applies to cases where the context can bear generality.
This is a secondary source, not a primary text of the Qur'an or mutawatir hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم شرع المصنف له في التقسيم الرابع فقال:
بيان عبارة النص]
وأما الاستدلال بعبارة النص، فهو العمل بظاهر ما سيق الكلام له، إنما عد الاستدلال من أقسام النظم تسامحا؛ لأنه فعل المستدلّ، والذي هو من أقسام الكناية أي الاستدلال.
حد الزنا : فإنّه ليس بإقرار بالزنا؛ إذ يمكن أن يكون المراد بالجماع المباشرة الفاحشة. (القمر)
لا يحد حد الزنا إلخ وإنما لم يحدّ هذا المصدّق وإن وجد الإقرار التزاما؛ لأنّ تصديق الزنا قبل وجود الزنا لا يتصوّر، فكأنه لم يُقرّ. (السنبلي) بخلاف ما إذا إلخ: هذا جواب لسؤال مقدّر، تقريره: أنه يشكل هذا القانون أي أنّ الكناية لا يثبت بها العقوبات بما لو قذف رجل رجلاً بالزنا، فقال الثالث هو كما قلت، فإن الثالث يُحدّ مع أنه ليس بتصريح بالنسبة إلى الزنا؟ وخلاصة الدفع أنّ كاف التشبيه يوجب العموم في المحل الذي يحتمله، حتى قلنا في قول علي : "إنما أعطينهم الذمة وبذلوا الجزية ليكون دماءهم كدمائنا وأموالهم كأموالنا": إنه يجري العموم فيما يسقط بالشبهات وفيما يثبت بها؛ فهذا الكاف أيضًا يوجب العموم؛ لأنه حصل في محلّ يحتمله، فكان نسبته إلى الزنا قطعا كما هو موجب العام . (السنبلي) يحدّ هذا المصدّق أي الآخر، ولو قذف رجلا بالزنا، فقال الثالث: صدقت في قولك هذا، يحدّ هذا الثالث للصراحة كذا قال بحر العلوم (القمر) يوجب العموم ولما كان كذلك فلا إبهام فيه؛ لأنّه تقرّر عند العلماء أنّ تناول العام إلى أفراده قطعية؛ فلا يكون كنايات. (القمر) وإنما عد الاستدلال إلخ جواب سؤال مقدّر تقريره : أن هذا البحث لأقسام النظم فكيف أورد فيه المصنف رحيم أقسام الاستدلال؛ لأنّ الاستدلال صفة المستدلّ لا صفة النظم، وإنّما صفة النظم هو ذات عبارة النص وهو ليس بمقصود ههنا؟ فأجاب بأنّ عد الاستدلال من أقسام النظم تسامح. (السنبلي)
Then the author proceeded to the fourth division and said: Explanation of 'Ibarat al-Nass. As for reasoning by the explicit textual statement (istidlal bi 'ibarat al-nass), it is acting upon the apparent meaning for which the speech was intended. It is only that reasoning has been counted among the divisions of textual structure (nazm) by way of tolerance, because it is the act of the one who reasons, and that which is from the divisions of kinayah — i.e., reasoning. (Then commentary explains examples about hadd for zina and qadhf, and that the kaf of comparison creates generality, and why reasoning is counted in the discussion of structural divisions despite being an act.)
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage begins the fourth division of the text, which is about reasoning from the explicit meaning of a textual statement ('ibarat al-nass). The author clarifies that such reasoning, though it is the activity of the mujtahid, is included under the categories of textual structure (nazm) for convenience. The glosses provide further explanation: the earlier examples about kinayah and hudud are revisited. Specifically, the statement 'I had illicit sex' is not an acknowledgment (iqrar) for zina because the word 'jama'a' could mean mere touching, not intercourse. Similarly, if someone says 'you lied' to a confession before the actual act, it is ambiguous. However, if a third party says 'it is as you said' (using kaf tashbih), this is considered a general statement (amm) establishing the meaning explicitly, so it is no longer kinayah. The glossator al-Sanbali explains the rationale for counting reasoning as part of nazm: the discussion is about divisions of nazm, so reasoning is included loosely because it involves the understanding of the text's structure. The citation from 'Bahr al-Ulum' supports the point about explicit language.

Key Terms
عبارة النص ('ibarat al-nass): The explicit wording of a text from which a ruling is directly derived, acting upon its apparent meaning (zahir).
الاستدلال (al-istidlal): Reasoning or inference from a text to derive a legal ruling.
النظم (al-nazm): The structural arrangement of a text, including its wording and composition, as a subject of analysis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what effect does the kaf of comparison (kaf al-tashbih) have in the phrase 'huwa kama qulta'?
A) It creates ambiguity, making the statement kinayah.
B) It creates generality, making the statement explicit (sarih).
C) It has no effect on the legal classification.
D) It restricts the meaning to a specific case.

Key Takeaway
The kaf of comparison in a statement makes it a general term, rendering the statement explicit (sarih) rather than kinayah, and thereby imposing legal consequences such as hadd.

Al-Quran as Source
intermediate

Dalālat al-Naṣṣ: The Definitive Proof for Ḥudūd and Kaffārāt
Explaining the definitive nature of dalālat al-naṣṣ and its application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why dalālat al-naṣṣ is considered a qaṭʿī proof, distinguish it from qiyās with a deduced cause, and apply it to derive rulings for specific cases such as stoning and expiation.

Core Idea
Dalālat al-naṣṣ is a qaṭʿī proof; it alone can establish ḥudūd and kaffārāt, not ẓannī qiyās.

In Hanafi usul, not all proofs carry the same weight. When it comes to fixed punishments (ḥudūd) and expiations (kaffārāt), the school requires definitive certainty. This lesson unpacks the fourth division of textual indication, showing why dalālat al-naṣṣ qualifies as qaṭʿī, while analogy with a deduced cause does not. You'll see how this principle is applied to classical cases like stoning for zinā, highway robbery, and a woman's expiation for breaking her fast.

Guided Path
1
Trace the objection and response
Start by examining the hypothetical objection: if Hell is the sufficient recompense for intentional murder, why are diya and qiṣāṣ still required? Follow how the Hanafi response distinguishes between recompense of the act (Hell) and recompense of the subject (diya/qiṣāṣ as rights of the victim's heirs). This clarifies that there is no conflict.
The response relies on another text (Qurʾān 5:45) establishing qiṣāṣ, showing that multiple proofs can coexist.
2
Identify why dalālat al-naṣṣ is qaṭʿī
The text states that ḥudūd and kaffārāt require a definitive proof (qaṭʿī) because they are warded off by doubts. Dalālat al-naṣṣ is qaṭʿī because the indication is drawn directly from the text itself, not subject to ijtihād. Contrast this with qiyās using a deduced cause (ʿilla mustanbaṭa), which is ẓannī.
This is a Hanafi specificity: dalālat al-naṣṣ is a separate category from ʿibārat al-naṣṣ.
3
Distinguish between two types of qiyās
Note the critical exception: qiyās with a textual cause (ʿilla manṣūṣa) equals dalālat al-naṣṣ in definitiveness. This is because the cause is explicitly mentioned in the text, removing speculation. Examples include the prohibition of sodomy analogized to menstruation via the explicit cause of 'harm' (Qurʾān 2:222).
This nuance prevents the common mistake of thinking all qiyās is too weak for ḥudūd.
4
Apply to the given examples
Work through each example: (a) Stoning a muḥṣan adulterer other than Māʿiz is established via dalālat al-naṣṣ from the Māʿiz story and also by an explicit abrogated recitation verse. (b) The ḥadd of highway robbery for an accomplice (radʾ) is derived from the word 'sowing corruption' in Qurʾān 5:33. (c) A woman's expiation for intentional intercourse while fasting is derived via dalālat al-naṣṣ from the hadith of the man, because enabling is an act.
These cases show how dalālat al-naṣṣ functions as a qaṭʿī proof in practice.

(القمر)
The explanation of the textual indication (dalālat al-naṣṣ). Explanation of the divisions. The fourth division: And it cannot be said: If it were so, then blood-money (diya) and retaliation (qiṣāṣ) would not be obligatory upon him. For we say: That is the recompense of the subject (the victim), while the recompense of the act is the expiation (kaffāra) in case of error, and Hell in case of intentional murder. Even if that is granted, retaliation is established by another text. For this reason, it is correct to establish the ḥudūd and expiations through textual indication (dalālat al-nuṣūṣ) and not through analogy (qiyās). That is, because textual indication is definitive (qaṭʿī) and analogy is speculative (ẓannī). It is correct to establish the ḥudūd and expiations by the former and not the latter. This is when the analogy uses a deduced cause (ʿilla mustanbaṭa). But if it uses a textual cause (ʿilla manṣūṣa), then it equals textual indication in definitiveness and proof. Example of establishing ḥudūd through textual indication: establishing the penalty of stoning for fornication (zinā) against someone other than Māʿiz (for whom the textual expression established it). "If it were so, etc.": That is, if the complete and sufficient recompense for the intentional killer were Hell, then blood-money and retaliation would not be obligatory upon the intentional killer in this world. But the necessary consequence is false, for the free person is killed for the free person, and for the slave, and the father is not killed if he kills his son intentionally; rather, blood-money is due from his wealth—as in al-Durr al-Mukhtār. So the antecedent is likewise false. Thus it is known that the meaning of His statement (Qurʾān 5:95) "then a recompense" is the recompense of the Hereafter. So the meaning is that all of the Hereafter recompense for the intentional killer is Hell; there is no harm if the obligation of expiation upon him is from the recompense of this world. (al-Qamar) "For we say, etc." The summary is that what is meant is the complete and sufficient recompense, but what is mentioned in the verse is the recompense of the act, which is Hell in case of intentional killing, no more. As for blood-money or retaliation, it is the recompense of the subject (the victim), for it is the right of the heirs of the victim; so their establishment does not harm the intended complete and sufficient recompense. (al-Qamar) "That" (i.e., that retaliation is the recompense of the act) is established by another text, namely His statement: "And We prescribed for them therein that the soul for the soul, the eye for the eye, the nose for the nose" (Qurʾān 5:45) the verse. In it, since retaliation is added by the expression of the text concerning retaliation, the indication of His statement (Qurʾān 4:93) "then his recompense is Hell" becomes abandoned; so it is permissible to add the obligation of expiation upon the intentional killer through the textual indication of the text concerning unintentional killing. So reflect. (al-Qamar) "It is correct to establish, etc.": For it is necessary to have a definitive proof to establish ḥudūd and expiations because they are warded off by doubts, and analogy is a proof that contains doubt. (al-Qamar) "With a deduced cause, etc.": An example is the analogy of the prohibition of excess in plaster and lime by the cause of quantity and weight on the prohibition of the six items derived from his statement (Ṣaḥīḥ) "wheat for wheat, barley for barley, etc." An example of analogy with a textual cause is the analogy of the prohibition of sodomy (liwāṭ) on the prohibition of intercourse during menstruation by the cause of harm mentioned in His statement: "Say: It is harm, so keep away from women during menstruation" (Qurʾān 2:222) (al-Sunbulī). "So it equals, etc." As has passed at the beginning of the book, so remember. (al-Qamar) "Which is established, etc.": A description of Māʿiz. It was narrated by al-Tirmidhī from Abū Hurayra (may Allāh be pleased with him) that Māʿiz al-Aslamī came to the Messenger of Allāh (peace be upon him)… the story of Māʿiz (may Allāh be pleased with him) is recorded in Bukhārī no. 497, Muslim no. 1691, and Tirmidhī no. 1428. (Explanation of the fourth division) Because Māʿiz (may Allāh be pleased with him) was only stoned because he was an adulterer who was muḥṣan, not because he was Māʿiz or a companion. So everyone who is like that is stoned. But stoning is also established for every muḥṣan adulterer by another text. And establishing the ḥadd of highway robbery (qaṭʿ al-ṭarīq) for one who is a helper (radʾ) for them by the textual indication of His statement: "And they strive in the land causing corruption" (Qurʾān 5:33). And an example of establishing expiations through textual indication: establishing the expiation upon a woman who intentionally had intercourse during the day in Ramaḍān by the textual indication of a text reported... (He said: He has committed zinā, so he turned away from him, then he came from his other side and said: He has committed zinā, so he turned away from him, then he came from the other side and said: O Messenger of Allāh, he has committed zinā, so he ordered it on the fourth time, and he was taken out to the stoning place and stoned with stones. (al-Qamar) "Was like that": i.e., an adulterer who was muḥṣan, and you have known the meaning of iḥṣān, so remember. The method of stoning is as in al-Durr al-Mukhtār: The muḥṣan is stoned in an open space until he dies, and they line up like rows of prayer for stoning him; whenever a group stoned they step back and others stone. (al-Qamar) "But, etc." The purpose is to repel a misunderstanding arising from the previous speech, namely: Is the establishment of the stoning penalty for fornication for someone other than Māʿiz, if he commits fornication and is muḥṣan, only by textual indication and not by other? The summary of the rebuttal is that stoning is established for every muḥṣan adulterer also by the textual expression (ʿibārat al-naṣṣ), and there is no harm in a ruling being established by two proofs: the textual indication and the textual expression. (al-Qamar) "By another text": This is the verse whose recitation is abrogated but its ruling remains: "The old man and the old woman, if they commit zinā, then stone them as a punishment from Allāh." And what is meant by old man and old woman is the muḥṣan man and muḥṣan woman, as in al-Mirqāt. (al-Qamar) "And establishing, etc." is in the nominative case, coordinated with "establishing the ḥadd" etc. (al-Qamar) "Radʾan lahum": i.e., helpers of highway robbers. In al-Qāmūs: al-radʾ (with kasra) is al-ʿawn (helper). (al-Qamar) "By the textual indication of His statement" and strive etc. For the expression of the text imposes a ḥadd upon the combatants (muḥāribūn). The form of muḥāraba is directly engaging in fighting, and its linguistic meaning is overpowering the enemy and frightening in a way that blocks the road. The helper (radʾ) also has this meaning, so he is like a combatant. Thus it has been said. (al-Qamar) "Upon a woman, etc." What is said: that the woman did not perform an act, and that the woman is only the locus of the man's act, in response: that the woman enabling the man for intercourse is her act. (al-Qamar) "By the textual indication of a text reported, etc." It is narrated by Bukhārī that Abū Hurayra said: While we were sitting with the Prophet (peace be upon him), a man came and said: O Messenger of Allāh, I am destroyed. He said: What is the matter? He said: I had intercourse with my wife while I was fasting. He said: And do you have a slave to free? He said: No. He said: Can you fast two consecutive months? He said: No. He said: Can you feed sixty poor people? He said: No. He said: Sit down. The Prophet (peace be upon him) waited, and while we were like that, a large basket of dates was brought to the Prophet. He said: Where is the questioner? He said: I am. He said: Take this and give it in charity. The man said: To someone poorer than me, O Messenger of Allāh? By Allāh, there is no household between the two lava plains (i.e., the two black stony tracts) poorer than my household. The Prophet (peace be upon him) laughed until his incisors could be seen, then said: Feed it to your family. (al-Qamar).
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharḥ) on the text of Noor al-Anwar, explaining the fourth division of textual indication (dalālat al-naṣṣ). The author first addresses an objection: if the complete recompense for intentional murder is Hell, then why are diya and qiṣāṣ still required in this world? The response distinguishes between the recompense of the act (which is Hell for intentional murder) and the recompense of the victim (which is diya/qiṣāṣ as a right of the heirs). Retaliation (qiṣāṣ) is established by another text (Qurʾān 5:45), so there is no conflict. The key point is that ḥudūd and kaffārāt can be established through dalālat al-naṣṣ because it is a definitive proof (qaṭʿī), whereas analogy (qiyās) with a deduced cause is speculative (ẓannī) and cannot establish such penalties due to the principle that ḥudūd are warded off by doubts. However, if the analogy uses a textual cause (ʿilla manṣūṣa), it equals dalālat al-naṣṣ in definitiveness. Examples include: stoning a muḥṣan adulterer other than Māʿiz (using the example of Māʿiz and the abrogated stoning verse), establishing the ḥadd of highway robbery for an accomplice (radʾ) using the verse on ḥirāba (Qurʾān 5:33), and the expiation for a woman who intentionally has intercourse during Ramadan, derived from the hadith of the man who broke his fast by intercourse. The commentary also clarifies that stoning is established both by dalālat al-naṣṣ and by an explicit text (the abrogated recitation verse). The Hanafi position is that such proofs are sufficient for ḥudūd and kaffārāt because of their definitiveness.

Key Terms
دلالة النص (dalālat al-naṣṣ): Textual indication: the meaning derived from a text beyond its literal expression, based on the context or purpose of the text. Here, it is defined as a definitive (qaṭʿī) proof.
قياس (qiyās): Analogy: extending a ruling from an original case to a new case due to a shared cause (ʿilla). Here, it is divided into qiyās with a deduced cause (ẓannī) and qiyās with a textual cause (qaṭʿī).
علة مستنبطة (ʿilla mustanbaṭa): A deduced cause: a cause derived by ijtihād rather than explicitly stated in the text. Such qiyās is speculative (ẓannī).
علة منصوصة (ʿilla manṣūṣa): A textual cause: a cause explicitly mentioned in the primary text. Such qiyās is definitive (qaṭʿī) like textual indication.
حد (ḥadd): A fixed punishment prescribed by the Qurʾān or Sunnah for major sins (e.g., stoning for zinā, cutting of hand for theft). These are established only by definitive proofs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can dalālat al-naṣṣ establish ḥudūd and kaffārāt while qiyās with a deduced cause (ʿilla mustanbaṭa) cannot?
A) Because dalālat al-naṣṣ is a direct quote from the Quran, while qiyās is not.
B) Because dalālat al-naṣṣ is a definitive (qaṭʿī) proof, while qiyās with a deduced cause is speculative (ẓannī).
C) Because qiyās is always prohibited in matters of ḥudūd and kaffārāt.
D) Because the Companions only used dalālat al-naṣṣ for such cases.

Key Takeaway
Dalālat al-naṣṣ is a definitive proof equivalent to explicit text, enabling the establishment of ḥudūd and kaffārāt, unlike speculative qiyās with a deduced cause.

Refuting the Invalid Analogy: Zakat on a Minor in Hanafi Usul
Refuting objection that Hanafi zakat ruling uses invalid analogy from sentence structure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the invalid analogy in the dispute about zakat on a minor and explain the Hanafi refutation based on textual evidence and the principle of sentence conjunction.

Core Idea
Hanafis rely on direct hadith, not analogy from prayer, for the ruling on zakat for a minor.

In this passage, the author addresses a common accusation against the Hanafi school: that their ruling on zakat for a minor is based on a flawed analogy from the conjunction of prayer and zakat in the Quran. The refutation demonstrates two key usul principles: the primacy of textual evidence (nas) over analogy (qiyas), and the grammatical principle that complete sentences conjoined do not necessitate sharing. This lesson will walk you through the objection, the Hanafi counter-argument, and the underlying linguistic and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested ruling and the accusation
The passage begins by stating the Hanafi ruling: zakat is not obligatory on a minor. It then mentions an implied objection that this ruling is derived from an invalid analogy based on the conjunction of zakat and prayer in the Quran.
The objection is hypothetical but reflects a real criticism of the Hanafi school.
2
Examine the Hanafi proof from hadith
The author immediately clarifies that the Hanafi ruling is not based on any conjunction but on an explicit hadith: 'No zakat on the property of a minor.' This hadith is the primary textual evidence (nas).
The hadith is narrated by Ibn Mas'ud in Kitab al-Athar.
3
Analyze the grammatical analogy (complete vs. incomplete sentences)
The opponents had analogized from the grammar of sentence conjunction: just as an incomplete sentence shares its predicate with a preceding complete sentence, they claimed that zakat shares the ruling of prayer because of their pairing in the Quran. The Hanafis refute this by distinguishing between complete and incomplete sentences.
A complete sentence (jumlah taammah) does not require sharing; an incomplete sentence (jumlah naaqisah) does.
4
Understand the exception for suspended clauses
An exception occurs when a complete sentence is made conditional (suspended) by context, e.g., 'If you enter the house, you are divorced and my slave is free.' Here the second clause, though complete in effect, shares the condition because it is incomplete in suspension.
This shows that sharing only occurs when independence is lacking, not in normal conjunction.
5
Synthesize: the Hanafi position is based on nas, not faulty analogy
The Hanafi ruling stands on direct textual evidence, not on any grammatical or analogical inference. The grammatical discussion further blocks any false analogy from the Quranic pairing.
The hadith provides a clear and specific ruling that overrides any general analogy.

Section on mentioning invalid methods. And according to us also, zakat is not obligatory on the minor: "No zakat on the property of a minor". But not because of conjunction, rather because of his saying, peace be upon him: (No zakat on the property of a minor). And they considered it by the incomplete sentence, meaning these speakers analogized the complete sentence conjoined to a complete sentence like his saying: 'Zainab is divorced and Hind is divorced' with the incomplete sentence conjoined to a complete sentence like his saying: 'Zainab is divorced and Hind', for both necessarily share the predicate, so likewise the first two. And we said: The conjunction of a sentence to a sentence does not necessitate partnership; because partnership only occurs in the incomplete sentence due to its need for completion, which is the predicate. For Hind needed 'divorced'; hence partnership occurred in the incomplete sentence, contrary to the conjoined complete sentence, for it is complete. That is, between the conjunct and the conjoined. So if it is complete by itself, partnership is not required except in what it needs, like suspension in his statement: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced and my slave is free'; for the last sentence, though complete in effect, is incomplete in suspension. And 'according to us also' etc. is an answer to an implied objection that may be raised against us: you Hanafis too used this invalid method; you said that zakat is not obligatory on the minor because prayer is not obligatory on him, and the ruling of zakat is like the ruling of prayer due to their conjunction in the noble verse; so zakat is also not obligatory on him? And the answer is not hidden. (Al-Sunbuli): Not because of conjunction, i.e., not because of linking the two sentences in conjunction. (Al-Qamar): By the incomplete sentence; what is meant by the incomplete sentence is a single word which, when conjoined with what precedes it or with something else, becomes a complete sentence. (Al-Qamar): In the incomplete sentence, i.e., in conjoining the incomplete sentence to the complete one. And it is the predicate. I say: Perhaps what is meant is the predicate for example; because the incompleteness of a sentence is not necessarily due to lacking the predicate, but may be due to lacking the subject. (Al-Qamar): Partnership is not required etc., and the waw is for absolute partnership in the existence of the content of both sentences in reality. So their analogy of the complete sentence to the incomplete sentence is an analogy with a difference, which is the occurrence of necessity. (Al-Qamar): 'In effect' etc. means if someone said 'My slave is free' only, he would not need other words for effecting freedom. (Al-Sunbuli): 'Incomplete in suspension' because it is known by contextual indication that his intent is suspension, not immediate effect. (Al-Qamar). This wording is famous in books of fiqh, and Muhammad ibn al-Hasan narrated it in the Book of Athar, no. 297, chapter on zakat of gold and silver and the property of an orphan, from Ibn Mas'ud, may Allah be pleased with him, that he said: There is no zakat on the property of an orphan. So it becomes shared with it in suspension, contrary to his statement: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced and Zainab is divorced' for it does not suspend Zainab's divorce; because if his intent was suspension he would have said 'and Zainab' without mentioning the predicate; since the predicate of both sentences is one, so when he repeats it, it is known that his intent is immediate effect.

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of one of the invalid methods (al-wujuh al-fasida) and a rebuttal of an accusation against the Hanafi school. The author first states the Hanafi position: zakat is not obligatory on a minor. This is not derived from any analogy but directly from the hadith of the Prophet: 'No zakat on the property of a minor.' The opponents had accused the Hanafis of using a flawed analogy: just as in the sentence 'Zainab is divorced and Hind is divorced' the second sentence is incomplete and shares the predicate, so too the Hanafis allegedly analogized from the obligation of prayer (which is not on a child) to the obligation of zakat because they are conjoined in the Quranic verse. The author refutes this by clarifying that the Hanafi ruling is based on the hadith, not on any conjunction with prayer. Then he explains the principle of conjunction of sentences: when a complete sentence is conjoined to another complete sentence, there is no sharing (partnership) of elements because each is independent. However, when an incomplete sentence is conjoined, it shares the missing element. An exception is when the second sentence, though complete in effect (e.g., 'my slave is free'), is made conditional by context, then it shares the condition. The author then provides the detailed proof from the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud, which is the basis for the ruling. The discussion shows the Hanafi commitment to textual evidence over pure analogy.

Key Terms
زكاة (zakah): Obligatory alms on wealth under specified conditions
صبي (sabi): A minor child who has not reached puberty
جملة تامة (jumlah taammah): A sentence that is complete in meaning and does not require another element
عطف (ataf): Grammatical conjunction between two sentences or phrases

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because of analogy with prayer
B) Because of the hadith 'No zakat on the property of a minor'

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position is anchored in textual evidence (hadith), not analogy; the grammatical principle of sentence conjunction further refutes the opponent's flawed qiyas.

Does Prohibition Remain Under Duress? The Hanafi Position on Rukhsah
Explaining the dispute over remaining prohibition under rukhsah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi position on the status of prohibition under duress by analyzing the Quranic verse and applying the thamarah al-khilaf to an oath-breaking scenario.

Core Idea
Rukhsah removes punishment, not the prohibition itself.

This passage delves into a core usul dispute: when a person is compelled by necessity to commit a normally prohibited act, does the prohibition remain or is it lifted? The Hanafi school, citing Quran 2:173, argues that the verse's reference to 'forgiveness' (maghfirah) presupposes the existence of a prohibition, meaning only the punishment is excused. In contrast, the Sahibayn and al-Shafi'i interpret the same verse as lifting the prohibition entirely. This disagreement has direct practical consequences, as illustrated by the example of an oath not to eat haram.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Issue
Determine the core question: does the prohibition (hurmah) of an act remain when performed under duress (idtirar)?
The act in focus is drinking wine under duress.
2
State the Hanafi Position
According to Abu Hanifa, the prohibition remains but the person is not punishable. The act is still haram, but the sinner is excused.
This is derived from the word 'maghfirah' (forgiveness) in the verse.
3
Identify the Opposing View
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and al-Shafi'i hold that the prohibition is lifted; the act becomes halal under duress.
They interpret 'no sin' as meaning the act is permissible.
4
Analyze the Proof and Response
Both sides use Quran 2:173. The Hanafi response: forgiveness presupposes a prohibition, otherwise the term would be meaningless. The verse negates punishment, not prohibition.
The passage also notes that the necessity (idtirar) is assessed by ijtihad and must not be exceeded.
5
Apply the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical difference: if someone swears not to eat haram and then drinks wine under duress, according to the Hanafis the oath is not broken (since the wine remains haram but the duress excuses the act), while according to the opposing view the oath is broken (since the wine becomes halal).
This example shows how the theoretical dispute affects real rulings.

فصل في الأحكام المشروعة بيان الرخصة وأنواعها فهو من قبيل القسم الأول؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَنِ اضْطُرَّ غَيْرَ بَاغِ وَلَا عَادٍ فَلَا إِثْمَ عَلَيْهِ إِنَّ اللَّهَ غَفُورٌ رَحِيمٌ﴾ دلّ إطلاق المغفرة على قيام الحرمة والجواب أن إطلاق المغفرة باعتبار الاضطرار المرخص للتناول يكون بالاجتهاد، وعسى أن يقع التناول زائدا على قدر الحاجة؛ لأن من ابتلي بهذه المخمصة تعسّر عليه رعاية قدر الحاجة، وفائدة الخلاف تظهر فيما إذا حلف لا يأكل حرامًا ، فشرب خمرًا حال الاضطرار، فعندهما يحنث، وعندنا لا. لبقاء الحرمة لانتفاء الحرمة وسقوط غسل الرّجل في مدة المسح، فإن استتار القدم بالخف يمنع سراية الحدث إليه، وقد كان طاهراً، وما حل فوق الخفّف فقد زال بالمسح ، فلا يشرع الغسل في هذه المدة وإن بقي في حق غير اللابس، وهذا على رواية الأصوليين، وأما صاحب "الهداية" فقد قال: إن نزع الخفّ في المدة، وغسل الرّجل يكون مأجورًا. غير باغ إلخ: أي حال كونه غير باغ للذة وشهوة، ولا عادٍ أي متعد مقدار الحاجة. كذا في "المدارك".(القمر) على قيام الحرمة وعلى أن المنفي هو المؤاخذة. (القمر) يكون بالاجتهاد فإن المضطر يعلم بشهادة قلبه أنه مضطر. (القمر) على قدر الحاجة وهو ما به يحصل سد الرمق وبقاء الروح. (القمر) الخلاف: أي بيننا وبين أبي يوسف والشافعي حمل . (القمر) الرّجل: المراد بالرجل كل الجنس، وهما الرّجلان؛ إذ ليس غسل رجل ومسح رجل مشروعًا. (القمر) في مدة المسح وهي يوم وليلة للمقيم، وثلاثة أيام بلياليها للمسافر. (القمر) يمنع أي بالاعتبار الشرعي؛ فصار القدم حينئذ عند الشارع كالبطن والفخذ فلا يكون غسله مشروعًا؛ لأن سبب الغسل سراية الحدث إليه ولم يوجد (القمر) وقد كان إلخ : أي والحال أن الرجل قد كان قبل الحدث طاهرا، فإنه لَبِس الخف على طهارة كاملة وقت الحدث. (القمر) فلا يشرع الغسل إلخ: فلو غسل المتخفّف الرجل بدون نزع الخف بأن أدخل الرجل في الحوض مثلاً يكون آثماً، لأنه فعل ما ليس بمشروع له. (القمر) لأن الحكم الأصلي في المتخفّف هو الغسل، بل لو غسل المتخفف بدون نزع الخف في المدة أثم، لكن يلزم على هذا ما لو أدخل الرجل في الحوض بنية الغسل لا يجزيه ذلك الغسل، وقد ذكر في بعض الفتاوى أجزاء ذلك الغسل، والصحيح هو الذي بين الشيخ ابن الهمام له أن هذه الرواية غير صحيح، وما ذكر في "الهداية" من أولوية غسل الرجل فالمراد منه بعد نزع الخف وظاهر أن حكم المسح على هذا لم يبق أصلاً؛ لأن الحدث صار ساريًا في الرجل، وهذا لا ينافي كون الغسل سبب الإثم وقت عدم نزع الخف فافهم هذا ما في "التنوير". (السنبلي) يكون مأجوراً : لأن الغسل أشق، والعبادة الشاقة أكثر ثوابًا. (القمر)
Chapter on the legally prescribed rulings: Explanation of concession and its types. It is from the first category; due to His saying: 'But whoever is forced, neither desiring nor transgressing, there is no sin upon him. Indeed, Allah is Forgiving and Merciful.' The absolute nature of forgiveness indicates the establishment of prohibition. And the answer is that the absolute forgiveness is with consideration of the necessity that permits consumption and is by ijtihad, and perhaps consumption occurs exceeding the amount of need because whoever is afflicted with this starvation finds it difficult to observe the amount of need. And the benefit of the disagreement appears when someone swears not to eat anything haram and then drinks wine under duress. According to them [Abu Yusuf and Shafi'i], he breaks his oath; according to us, he does not. Due to the remaining of prohibition? Actually, due to the negation of prohibition? And the suspension of washing the foot during the period of wiping: because the covering of the foot by the khuff prevents the transmission of hadath to it, and it was pure, and what is above the khuff has been removed by wiping, so washing is not prescribed in this period even though it remains in the right of one who does not wear khuff. This is according to the narration of the usuliyyun. And as for the author of al-Hidayah, he said: removing the khuff during the period and washing the foot is rewarded. 'غير باغ' etc.: i.e., in the state of not seeking pleasure and desire, and not transgressing, i.e., exceeding the amount of need. As in al-Madarik. (al-Qamar) upon the establishment of prohibition and that what is negated is the punishment. (al-Qamar) is by ijtihad, for the one forced knows by the testimony of his heart that he is forced. (al-Qamar) according to the amount of need, which is what obtains the closing of the throat and survival of the soul. (al-Qamar) the disagreement: i.e., between us and Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i. (al-Qamar) the foot: by 'foot' is meant the entire genus, and they are two feet; since washing one foot and wiping one foot is not legislated. (al-Qamar) in the period of wiping, which is a day and a night for the resident and three days and nights for the traveler. (al-Qamar) prevents i.e., by legal consideration; thus the foot becomes at that time in the view of the Lawgiver like the belly and thigh, so its washing is not legislated because the cause of washing is the transmission of hadath to it and it did not occur. (al-Qamar) and it was... etc.: i.e., and the condition is that the foot was pure before the hadath, for he wore the khuff in a state of complete purity at the time of hadath. (al-Qamar) So washing is not legislated etc.: So if the one wearing khuff washes the foot without removing the khuff, e.g., by putting the foot in a basin, he is sinful because he did what is not legislated for him. (al-Qamar) because the original ruling for the one wearing khuff is washing, rather if he washes without removing the khuff during the period, he sins, but this necessitates that if he puts his foot in the basin with the intention of washing, that washing does not suffice him. It has been mentioned in some fatawa that it suffices, but the correct is that which Shaykh Ibn al-Humam explained: that this narration is not authentic. And what is mentioned in al-Hidayah about the preference of washing the foot is meant after removing the khuff. And it is apparent that the ruling of wiping on this has no basis, because the hadath has become transmitted to the foot, and this does not contradict that washing is a cause of sin at the time of not removing the khuff. Understand this. This is in al-Tanwir. (al-Sunbuli) is rewarded: because washing is more difficult, and difficult worship has more reward. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 492

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the types of legal rulings, specifically focusing on rukhsah (concession) and its implications. The author begins by citing Quran 2:173, which permits eating otherwise forbidden food under duress. The key legal dispute addressed is whether the prohibition remains during duress. According to Abu Hanifa, the prohibition remains, but the act is excused from punishment; thus, if someone swears an oath not to eat Haram and drinks wine under duress, he does not break his oath (since the wine remains Haram, but the oath was to avoid Haram, yet the duress makes it non-violative). The Sahibayn and Shafi'i hold that the prohibition is lifted, so the wine becomes Halal, and the oath is broken. The passage also discusses the issue of wiping over khuff (leather socks) during wudu, clarifying that during the period of wiping (one day for resident, three for traveler), washing the feet is not prescribed, and doing so without removing the khuff is sinful. The author distinguishes between the rulings for different scholarly positions.

Key Terms
الرخصة (al-rukhsah): A concession that permits an otherwise prohibited act due to necessity, while the prohibition remains in place.
الاضطرار (al-idtirar): A state of dire necessity that forces one to commit an otherwise prohibited act to preserve life.
الحرمة (al-hurmah): The legal prohibition of an act; the quality of being haram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, if a person swears not to eat haram and then drinks wine under duress, what is the ruling on the oath?
A) The oath is broken because the act becomes halal.
B) The oath is not broken because the act remains haram but the duress excuses the punishment.
C) The oath becomes invalid because duress nullifies oaths.
D) The oath is broken because the person committed a sin.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, rukhsah (concession) removes the punishment for an act under duress, but the prohibition (hurmah) remains intact, affecting the validity of oaths and other rulings.

The Cause of Kaffarah: Between Prohibition and Permission
Explanation of the cause (sabab) for kaffarah as a matter between prohibition and permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the cause (sabab) of kaffarah must be an act that is between prohibition and permission, and identify why purely prohibited or purely permissible acts do not suffice.

Core Idea
The cause of expiation must be an act that is both prohibited (by its consequence) and permissible (by its form) — a unique Hanafi usul insight.

In this passage from Noor al-Anwar, the author defines the cause of kaffarah as something 'dā'ir bayn al-ḥaẓr wa al-ibāḥa' — between prohibition and permission. This is because kaffarah combines worship (penance) and punishment (deterrence). We will unpack this definition with examples and examine a potential objection from tawbah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general causes for punishments
List the sabab for qisas, hadd, and kaffarah as mentioned in the passage. Note that while qisas and hudud have specific causes, kaffarah has a unique condition.
The passage clearly states: sabb al-qisas = qatl al-'amd; sabb hadd al-zina = zina; sabb qat' al-yad = sariqah.
2
Distinguish the nature of kaffarah
Understand why kaffarah is a 'type apart' (naw' ākhar). It combines elements of worship (ta'abbud) and punishment (uqubah). This dual nature imposes a special requirement on its cause.
Kaffarah is to be performed as an act of worship (e.g., fasting) but is obligatory due to a transgression.
3
Understand the condition: cause must be between prohibition and permission
Because kaffarah merges worship and punishment, its cause must reflect both qualities. The act must have a permissible side (to attach worship) and a prohibited side (to attach punishment).
The passage emphasizes: 'so that the worship is attached to the quality of permission, and the punishment is attached to the quality of prohibition.'
4
Apply to the examples given
Analyze the two examples: qatl khaṭā' (accidental killing) and ifṭār fī Ramaḍān (intentional break of fast). For each, identify the permissible aspect (hunting/consuming own property) and prohibited aspect (negligence/aggression against the fast).
The verse 4:92 is advanced as proof for the kaffarah of accidental killing.
5
Address the objection concerning tawbah
The commentary raises a potential counterexample: repentance (tawbah) is an act of worship caused by a purely prohibited act (sin). Explain why this does not contradict the rule: tawbah lacks the punitive element present in kaffarah.
This distinction is critical for defending the Hanafi position.

Chapter on the Causes of Legislated Rulings. Causes of punishments, hudud, and expiations. And kaffarah is another type: so the cause of qisas is intentional killing, the cause of hadd for zina is zina, the cause of amputation of the hand is theft. And the cause of kaffarah is a matter that is between prohibition and permission. That is because since it (kaffarah) is between worship and punishment, its cause must be a matter that is between prohibition and permission, so that the worship is attached to the quality of permission, and the punishment is attached to the quality of prohibition. Like killing by mistake: in terms of form, it is shooting at game which is permissible, but in terms of lack of caution it is prohibited because he hit a person and destroyed him; so kaffarah becomes obligatory. And intentionally breaking the fast in Ramadan: it is permissible in terms of consuming what one owns, and prohibited in terms of it being an aggression against the prescribed fast; so it is suitable as a cause for kaffarah. That is, unlawful and a major sin. (Then commentary follows.) And prohibited in terms of it being an aggression against the prescribed fast; so it is suitable to be a cause for kaffarah. That is, unlawful and a major sin.

Commentary
This passage from Noor al-Anwar explains the causes of various legal punishments and expiations. The text states that the cause of qisas is intentional murder, the cause of hadd for zina is zina, and the cause of amputation for theft is theft. It then focuses on kaffarah, stating that its cause must be something that lies between prohibition and permission, because kaffarah combines aspects of both worship and punishment. Examples include killing by mistake (qatl khata') and intentionally breaking the fast in Ramadan. The commentaries expand on these points, including a discussion on whether a purely prohibited act can be a cause for kaffarah, citing tawbah as a counterexample. The passage also quotes Quran 4:92 as evidence for the kaffarah of killing by mistake.

Key Terms
سبب (sabab): The cause that necessitates a legal ruling; here specifically the cause of kaffarah is an act that is between permission and prohibition.
الكفارة (al-kaffarah): An expiation that combines aspects of worship and punishment; its cause must be an act between permission and prohibition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The cause (sabab) of kaffarah must be an act that lies between prohibition and permission, such as accidental killing or intentional break of fast in Ramadan.

Proof of Obligation to Act on Khabar al-Wahid from Quran 9:122
Deriving the obligation to act on khabar al-wahid from Quran 9:122

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the obligation to act on khabar al-wahid from Quran 9:122 using Hanafi usul principles, and identify the exceptions to this obligation.

Core Idea
Khabar al-wahid obligates action because a single person's report is included in 'ta'ifa' and Allah commands its acceptance.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi proof from Quran 9:122 (the 'ta'ifa' verse) for the binding nature of solitary reports (khabar al-wahid). You will see how the linguistic generality of 'ta'ifa' and the imperative to warn yield a ruling that obligates the community to act upon such reports, while also noting the limitations: it does not produce certainty, and it fails if the matter is too widely known to be reliably reported by one person.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key term
Locate the word 'ta'ifa' in Quran 9:122. Recognize that this is the operative term in the Hanafi argument.
The verse: 'فَلَوْلا نَفَرَ مِنْ كُلِّ فِرْقَةٍ مِنْهُمْ طَائِفَةٌ...'
2
Analyze the linguistic scope
Determine the lexical meaning of 'ta'ifa'. According to Ibn Abbas and Hanafi linguists, it applies to one or more individuals. This makes the word 'amm' (general), covering a sole narrator.
Contrast with those who restrict 'ta'ifa' to multiple people.
3
Establish the obligation to accept
The verse commands the warned party to accept the warning: 'la'allahum yahdharun'. Since 'la'alla' cannot indicate hope for Allah, it is interpreted as a request (talab), making acceptance obligatory.
This is a key usul principle: figurative interpretation when literal meaning is impossible for Allah.
4
Derive the ruling and note exceptions
Combine the two points: the report can come from one person, and the community must accept it. Thus, khabar al-wahid obligates action (yujibu al-'amal). However, certainty is not produced (only zann), and the obligation is lifted for matters so widely known that a solitary report would be unreliable (e.g., jahr of basmalah).
The passage also mentions that a definitive qarinah (qarinah qat'iyyah) can yield certainty, but that is from the qarinah, not the report.

Obligation to act upon the Book and the Sunnah. And that it obligates action without certain knowledge of the Book, and it is His saying: 'Then why did not a group from every party go forth to gain understanding in religion and to warn their people when they return to them, that they might be cautious?' (Quran 9:122) i.e., why did not a small group from every large community go out of their homes to gain understanding in religion, meaning this small group goes to the scholars and travels in the horizons of the world to acquire knowledge, and to warn their remaining people at home for the sake of arranging livelihood and protecting family and wealth from the disbelievers, when this small group returns to this large group, so that they might also be cautious. Thus the pronoun of 'to gain understanding', 'to warn', and 'they return' refers to the small group, and 'their people' is the large group, and the pronoun 'to them' and 'that they might' refers to the large group. Allah obligated warning upon the small group, which is a noun for one and two or more, and obligated upon the large group to accept their statement and act upon it. So it is established that khabar al-wahid obligates action. In the verse there is another interpretation wherein these pronouns are reversed. Its conditions: It obligates action except when khabar al-wahid concerns something that repeatedly occurs and is widespread and many men are present at it, like the hadith of saying 'Bismillah' aloud, then it does not obligate action. (al-Qamar) It obligates action because it establishes overwhelming probability of truthfulness when the sufficient conditions for obligation are fulfilled. [Fath al-Ghaffar 1272] Without certain knowledge and without tranquility, for the single non-infallible person, even if he is just or a saint, may be affected by forgetfulness such that he does not distinguish between what he heard and what he did not hear, and thinks he heard what he did not hear, and reports it, or error. So how can his report be beneficial for certainty or tranquility? Yes, khabar al-wahid with a definitive qarinah yields certainty, as when someone reports the death of the sultan's son while the sultan is weeping with his companions and slapping his cheeks and there is great mourning. But certainty came from that qarinah, not from khabar al-wahid as such, and the discussion is about it. (al-Qamar) For one and two etc., according to what Ibn 'Abbas said. (al-Qamar) And He obligated, etc. This is based on that 'la'alla' originally indicates hope, which is impossible for Allah, so it is used figuratively to indicate request, because request is necessary for hope, so it indicates obligation. (al-Qamar) Reverse these pronouns: such that the pronoun of 'to gain understanding', 'to warn', and 'to them' refer to the large group, and the pronoun 'they return' and 'that they might' refer to the small group, and 'their people' is the small group. The meaning would then be: Why did not every large group send a small group to fight so that the large group remaining may gain understanding in religion and warn the small group (the outgoing ones) when they return to them, so that the small group might be cautious. In al-Tafsir al-Ahmadi, and the purpose is that not all people should go out to battle, so that understanding (i.e., the greater jihad) is not invalidated. (al-Qamar) End.
Source pages p. 505

Commentary
This passage uses Quran 9:122 to prove that khabar al-wahid obligates action. The verse commands a group (ta'ifa) to go forth and gain understanding, then warn their people. The word 'ta'ifa' can apply to one person or more, establishing that a report from a single person must be accepted and acted upon. The Hanafi commentator explains that the obligation falls on the warned group to accept the warning. The passage also clarifies that khabar al-wahid does not produce certainty (yaqin) or tranquility (tuma'ninah) because narrators are not infallible and may forget or err. However, if a definite circumstantial indicator (qarinah qat'iyyah) accompanies the report, certainty can result, but that is from the indicator, not the report itself. An exception is made: if the report concerns a widely known matter that many people would normally witness, like the jahr of basmalah in prayer, khabar al-wahid does not obligate action because such matters require widespread transmission. The commentator also discusses alternative interpretations of the verse's pronouns and the use of 'la'alla' (perhaps) to indicate obligation.

Key Terms
طائفة (ta'ifa): A group; lexically applicable to one or more, used here to prove that khabar al-wahid is included.
لعل (la'alla): A particle of hope or expectation; here interpreted figuratively as a request (talab) to indicate obligation.
قرينة قطعية (qarinah qat'iyyah): A definitive circumstantial indicator that, when accompanying khabar al-wahid, can produce certainty, but this certainty is attributed to the qarinah, not the report itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary argument from Quran 9:122 that Hanafis use to prove that khabar al-wahid obligates action?
A) The word 'firqah' (group) implies a large number.
B) The word 'ta'ifa' (party) can refer to one or more individuals, and Allah obligated the warned party to accept their warning.
C) The verse explicitly says 'accept the report of one person.'
D) The verse commands everyone to learn religion directly.

Key Takeaway
Khabar al-wahid obligates action (wujub al-'amal) based on Quran 9:122, but does not yield certainty; it suffices for practice when its conditions are met.

Proof for Khabar al-Wahid as Hujjah for Action
Proofs for hujjiyyah of khabar al-wahid from multiple sources

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four proofs (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, Reason) used by Hanafis to establish the hujjiyyah of khabar al-wahid and evaluate the counter-argument from those demanding certainty.

Core Idea
Khabar al-wahid obligates action even without certainty—proven by the Prophet's own practice and the Consensus of the Sahaba.

This lesson presents the Hanafi argument that a solitary report (khabar al-wahid) is a binding proof (hujjah) for practical rulings. The author draws on multiple lines of evidence: the Quranic obligation on scholars to make things clear, the Prophet's acceptance of gifts and single messengers, the Companions' unanimous acceptance of ahad reports, and rational necessity. We will walk through each proof and engage with a key objection based on the verse 'and do not pursue that of which you have no knowledge.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and its scope
Recognize that the claim is that khabar al-wahid is hujjah for action, not for producing certainty. Scope: all practical matters (ibadat, muamalat).
2
Examine the Quranic proof
The verse (Āl 'Imrān 3:187) commands those given knowledge to clarify and preach; this implies that the audience must accept their reports, even if from a single scholar.
The command is general ('amm), covering all reporters including ahad.
3
Examine the Sunnah proof
The Prophet accepted gifts based on a single report (e.g., from Salman) and sent single messengers (e.g., Mu'adh to Yemen, Dihyah to Caesar). These actions would be meaningless if the recipients were not obliged to act on the report of one.
His actions as a legislator indicate obligation.
4
Examine the Ijma proof
The Companions accepted ahad reports like 'The leaders are from Quraysh' and agreed on rulings about purity of water based on ahad reports, showing consensus.
Abu Bakr's use of the hadith at Saqifah and its acceptance without objection is a key example.
5
Examine the rational proof (al-Ma'qool)
Tawatur and shuhrah are not available for every incident; rejecting ahad would cause suspension of many legal rulings. Therefore, reason necessitates acceptance.
This is a form of necessity (darurah) maslaha.
6
Address the objection
The verse 'Do not pursue that of which you have no knowledge' (17:36) is cited by opponents. The Hanafi response: it applies to certainty in creed, not action; action upon zann is proven by Prophetic practice and Ijma.
See al-mukhtalaf_fih block for details.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب أقسام السنة وجوب العمل بالكتاب والسنة كلها، وحينئذٍ تكون ممّا نحن فيه على ما بيّنتُ ذلك في "التفسير الأحمدي"، ويمكن أن يكون المراد بالكتاب هو قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِذْ أَخَذَ اللَّهُ مِيثَاقَ الَّذِينَ أُوتُوا الْكِتَابَ لَتَبَيِّنَنَّهُ لِلنَّاسِ وَلا تَكتُمُونَهُ﴾، فقد أوجب على كل من أوتي علم الكتاب بيانه، ووعظه للناس، ولا فائدة منه إلا قبول الناس تلك الموعظة، فيكون خبر الواحد حجة للعمل. والسنة، وهي أنه عليه السلام قبل الهدية، وخبر سلمان في الهدية حتى أخذها وأكلها، وأيضا بعث عليا ومعاذا إلى اليمن بالقضاء، ودحية الكلبي إلى قيصر روم برسالة كتاب يدعوه إلى الإسلام، فلو لم يكن أخبار الآحاد موجبة للعمل لما فعل ذلك، خبر بريرة في الصدقة حتى قال في جوابها: "لكِ صدقة". وهذه الأخبار وإن كانت آحادًا لكن لما تلقته الأمة بالقبول صارت بمنزلة المشهور فلا يلزم إثبات أخبار الآحاد. ووقع في بعض النسخ قوله: والإجماع والمعقول عطفا على الكتاب والسنة، فالإجماع هو أن الصحابة احتجوا بأخبار الآحاد فيما بينهم، واحتج أبو بكر على الأنصار بقوله عليه السلام: "الأئمة من قريش" فقبلوه من غير نكير، وهكذا أجمعوا على قبول خبر الآحاد في طهارة الماء ونجاسته. والمعقول: هو أن المتواتر والمشهور لا يوجدان في كل حادثة، فلو رد خبر الواحد فيها لتعطلت الأحكام. وقيل: لا عمل إلا عن علم بالنص، وهو قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا تَقْفُ مَا لَيْسَ لَكَ بِهِ عِلْمٌ﴾ أي يقين.
Chapter on the Divisions of Sunnah. The obligation of acting upon the Book and the Sunnah entirely. And then it is from our subject as I explained in 'al-Tafsir al-Ahmadi'. It is possible that what is meant by the Book is His statement, exalted: 'And when Allah took a covenant from those who were given the Scripture, that you must make it clear to the people and not conceal it.' So He obligated upon everyone who has been given knowledge of the Book to clarify it and preach it to the people, and there is no benefit from it except the people's acceptance of that sermon, so the solitary report is a proof for action. And the Sunnah: that he, peace be upon him, accepted the gift, and the report of Salman about the gift until he took it and ate it, and also he sent Ali and Mu'adh to Yemen for judgment, and Dihyah al-Kalbi to the Caesar of Rome with a written message inviting him to Islam. So if solitary reports were not obligating action, he would not have done that. The report of Burayrah about charity until he said in response to her: 'It is charity for you.' And these reports, even though they are solitary, once the Ummah received them with acceptance, they become like the mashhur, so it is not necessary to prove the solitary reports. And in some copies it says: 'and consensus and reason' as conjunctives to the Book and the Sunnah. So consensus: that the Companions argued with solitary reports among themselves, and Abu Bakr argued against the Ansar with his statement, peace be upon him: 'The leaders are from Quraysh' and they accepted it without any disapproval. Similarly, they agreed upon accepting the solitary report regarding the purity and impurity of water. And reason: that the mutawatir and mashhur do not exist in every incident, so if the solitary report were rejected in them, rulings would be suspended. And it was said: action is only with knowledge from the text, which is His statement: 'And do not pursue that of which you have no knowledge,' i.e., certainty.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage is an extended proof (dalil) for the binding authority of khabar al-wahid in matters of action, as opposed to creed. The author begins by establishing that the obligation upon scholars to clarify the Book necessitates that the audience accepts their clarification, which extends to accepting the report of a single trustworthy narrator. He then provides examples from the Prophet's own practice: accepting gifts and sending single messengers to individuals and nations. These actions would be meaningless if the recipients were not obliged to act upon the report of one. The author anticipates an objection: these proofs themselves reach us through ahad reports. He answers that these particular ahad reports have been accepted by the Ummah (talaqqat bi'l-qabul), raising them to the level of mashhur, so they do not rely on mere ahad. He next cites two additional proofs from some manuscripts: Ijma (consensus) and Ma'qool (rational reasoning). The Ijma proof is illustrated by Abu Bakr's use of the hadith 'The leaders are from Quraysh' to argue against the Ansar during the Saqifah incident; the Companions accepted it without objection, showing their consensus on accepting ahad reports. The rational proof: not every incident can be proven by tawatur or shuhrah, so rejecting ahad would cause a cessation of legal rulings. Finally, he refutes the objection of those (like Ibn Dawood) who demand certainty ('ilm yaqin) for all rulings, citing the verse 'Do not pursue that of which you have no knowledge,' arguing that this refers to certainty of belief, not action.

Key Terms
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wahid): A hadith that does not reach the level of tawatur but is narrated by one or more narrators in each generation, falling short of shuhrah.
تلقته الأمة بالقبول (talaqqathu al-ummah bi'l-qabul): A hadith that the ummah has universally accepted and acted upon, elevating it to the level of mashhur even if the chain is ahad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What was the basis of the rational proof (al-Ma'qool) for accepting khabar al-wahid?
A) Necessity: tawatur and shuhrah are not available for every incident, so rejecting ahad would suspend rulings.
B) The Prophet's personal practice of accepting gifts.
C) The consensus of the four imams.
D) The Quranic verse 'Do not pursue that of which you have no knowledge.'

Key Takeaway
Khabar al-wahid is a binding proof for action due to the Quran, the Sunnah, the Ijma of the Companions, and rational necessity, as long as it does not conflict with stronger evidence.
